(рдирд╡реАрди рдирд╛рдЯрдХрдореН)

рдХрд▓рд┐рдХрд╛рддрд╛рд╕реНрде рд░рд╛рдЬрдХреАрдп-рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрдд рдорд╣рд╛рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рд▓рдпрд╛рдиреНрдпрддрд░-

рд╢реНрд░реАрдХрд╛рд▓реАрдкрдж-рддрд░реНрдХрд╛рдЪрд╛рд░реНрдпреЗрдг

резреорелрезрддрдореЗ рд╢рдХрд╛рдмреНрджреЗ
рдХрд▓рд┐рдХрд╛рддрд╛рдиреНрддрд░реНрдЧрдд-рд╢реНрдпрд╛рдорд╡рд╛рдЬрд╛рд░рд╕реНрде-
рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрдд-рд╕рд╛рд╣рд┐рддреНрдп- рдкрд░рд┐рд╖рджрд╛ рдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрдореН

рдореВрд▓реНрдпрдореНтАФрдкрд░рд┐рд╖рддреН рд╕рджрд╕реНрдпрд╛рдирд╛рдореНтАФ111реж рджреНрд╡рд╛рджрд╢рд╛рдирдХрдореН
рдЕрдиреНрдпреЗрд╖рд╛рдореНтАФрезрел рд░реВрдкреНрдпрдХрдорд╛рддреНрд░рдореН

рдХрд▓рд┐рдХрд╛рддрд╛рд░рд╛рдЬрдХреАрдпрд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрдорд╣рд╛рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рд▓рдпрд╛рдзреНрдпрд╛рдкрдХ-
рд╢реНрд░реАрдпреБрдХреНрддрдХрд╛рд▓реАрдкрдж- рддрд░реНрдХрд╛рдЪрд╛рд░реНрдпрдкреНрд░рдгреАрдд-рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдердорд╛рд▓рд╛

рдирд┐рдГрд╢реЗрд╖рдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрд╛ рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдерд╛тАФ

резред рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд┐рджреАрдкрд┐рдХрд╛ (рдЬрдЧрджреАрд╢рдХреГрддрдкреНрд░рд╢рд╕реНрддрдкрд╛рджрднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрд╕реВрдХреНрддреЗ рдЯреАрдХрд╛) рд╡рдЩреНрдЧрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдордпрднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрддрд╛рддреНрдкрд░реНрдпрд╛рджрд┐рд╕рдореБрдкрд╡реГрд╣рд┐рддрд╛ред

реиред рдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рджреАрдкрд┐рдХрд╛ (рдЧрджрд╛рдзрд░рднрдЯреНрдЯрд╛рдЪрд╛рд░реНрдпрдХреГрддрдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рд╡рд╛рджрд╕реНрдп рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрд╢рд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд░реАрдпрдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдерд╕реНрдп рдЯреАрдХрд╛) рд╡рдЩреНрдЧрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдордп-рдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рд╡рд╛рдж-рддрд╛рддреНрдкрд░реНрдпрд╡рд┐рд╡рд░рдг рдирд╛рдирд╛рджрд░реНрд╢рдирдорддрд╕рд┐рджреНрдзрдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рддрддреНрддреНрд╡ рд╡рд┐рдЪрд╛рд░рд╛рддреНрдордХрдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рд╡рд╛рджрднреВрдорд┐рдХрд╛рджрд┐рд╕рдорд▓рдЩреНрдХреГрддрд╛ред

рейред рд░рддреНрдирд▓рдХреНрд╖реНрдореАрдЯреАрдХрд╛ (рдХрдгрд╛рджрддрд░реНрдХрд╡рд╛рдЧреАрд╢рдХреГрддрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рд░рддреНрдирд╛рдЦреНрдпрдиреИрдпрд╛рдпрд┐рдХрдкрджрд╛рд░реНрде рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдердЯреАрдХрд╛) рднрд╛рд╖рд╛рд░рддреНрдирднреВрдорд┐рдХрд╛-рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрд╢рд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд░реАрдпрдкрд╛рд░рд┐рднрд╛рд╖рд┐рдХрдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╡рд┐рд╡реГрддрд┐рд╡рд┐рднреВрд╖рд┐рддрд╛ред

рекред рд╕рд╛рд░рдордЮреНрдЬрд░реАрдЯреАрдХрд╛ (рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдЧреНрд░рдиреНрде)ред

релред рд╕рд╛рдЦреНрдпрд╕рд╛рд░рдЯреАрдХрд╛ред

ремред рд╕рд╛рдЦреНрдпрдХрд╛рд░рд┐рдХрд╛рдЧреМрдбрдкрд╛рджрднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрдЯреАрдХрд╛ред

репред рдорд╛рд▓рд╡рд┐рдХрд╛рдЧреНрдирд┐рдорд┐рддреНрд░рдЯреАрдХрд╛ред

резрезред рдирд▓рджрдордпрдиреНрддреАрдпрдореН (рд╕рдкреНрддрд╛рдЩреНрдХрдирд╛рдЯрдХрдореН)ред

резреиред рд╕реНрдпрдордиреНрддрдХреЛрджреНрдзрд╛рд░ (рдПрдХрд╛рдЩреНрдХреЛ рд╡реНрдпрд╛рдпреЛрдЧ)ред

резрейред рдкреНрд░рд╢рд╛рдиреНрддрд░рддреНрдирд╛рдХрд░рдореН ( рдирд╡рд╛рдЩреНрдХрдирд╛рдЯрдХрдореН )ред

резрекред рд╢реИрд╢рд╡рд╕рд╛рдзрдирдореН (рдЦрдгреНрдбрдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпрдореН рдкреНрд░реЗрдордкреБрд╖реНрдкрд╕рд╢рд╕реНрдХреГрддрдкрддреНрд░рд┐рдХрд╛рдпрд╛ рдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрдореН)ред

рдЕрд╢рдд рд╕рд╕реНрдХреГрддрдкрддреНрд░рд┐рдХрд╛рджреМ рдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрд╛ рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдерд╛тАФ

резред рд╕рддреНрдпрд╛рдиреБрднрд╛рд╡рдореН (рдорд╣рд╛рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпрдореН)

реиред рдЬрд╛рддрд┐рдмрд╛рдзрдХрд╡рд┐рдЪрд╛рд░ (рдиреНрдпрд╛рдпрдЧреНрд░рдиреНрде)ред

рейред рдпреБрдЧрд▓рд╛рдЩреНрдЧреБрд░реАрдпрдореН (рд╡рдЩреНрдХрд┐рдордЪрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╕реНрдп рдпреБрдЧрд▓рд╛рдЩреНрдЧреБрд░реАрдпрдХрдерд╛рдиреБрд╡рд╛рдж)ред

рекред рдЖрд╢реБрддреЛрд╖рд╛рд╡рджрд╛рдирдореН (рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпрдореН)ред

рддрдерд╛

рдЖрд░реНрдпрдкреНрд░рднрд╛ рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпреЛрджрдп-рдордЮреНрдЬреВрд╖рд╛ рдкреНрд░реЗрдордкреБрд╖реНрдк-рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрд╕рд╛рд╣рд┐рддреНрдпрдкрд░рд┐рд╖рддреН рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрдкрджреНрдпрд╡рд╛рдгреА ‘рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрдХрд▓реЗрдЬ рдореНрдпрд╛рдЧрд╛рдЬрд┐рдиреН’ рдкреНрд░рднреГрддрд┐рдкрддреНрд░рд┐рдХрд╛рд╕реБ рдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрд╛ рд╕рдЦреНрдпрд╛рддреАрддрд╛ рд╡рд┐рд╡рд┐рдзрд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╛ рдЧрджреНрдпрдкрджреНрдпрдордпрдирд┐рдмрдиреНрдзрд╛ред

рдЕрдкреНрд░рдХрд╛рд╢рд┐рддрд╛рдирд╛ рдиреЗрд╣ рдирд╛рдорд╛рдирд┐ рд╕рдореБрд▓реНрд▓рд┐рдЦреНрдпрдиреНрддреЗред

рд╡рд┐рд╡рд┐рдзрд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рдкрд╛рд░рд╛рд╡рд╛рд░рдкрд╛рд░рдЩреНрдЧрдо-рд╡рд┐рд╢реНрд╡рд╡рд┐рд╢реНрд░реБрддрдХреАрд░реНрддреНрддрд┐рдкреНрд░рддрд╛рди рдмрд╣реБрд╡рд┐рдзрд╡рд╛рдгреАрдкреНрд░рд╡рдЪрдирдкреНрд░рд╡реАрдгрдорд╣реЛрджрд╛рд░рдЪрд░рд┐рдд-рдПрдореН рдП рдкрд┐-рдПрдЗрдЪреН рдбрд┐ (рдХрд▓рд┐рдХрд╛рддрд╛), рдкрд┐-рдПрдЗрдЪреН рдбрд┐ (рдХреЗрдореНрдмреНрд░рд┐рдЬреН), рдПрдлреН рдЖрд░реН рдПрд╕реН рдПрд▓реН (рдЗ рд▓рдгреНрдб), рдПрдлреН рдП рдПрд╕реН (рдУрдпрд╛рд░реНрд╕), рдбрд┐ рд▓рд┐рдЯреН (рд░реЛрдо), рдЖрдЗ рдЗ рдПрд╕реН, рджрд░реНрд╢рдирд╡рд╛рдЧреАрд╢-рд╕рд░реНрд╡рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рд░рддреНрдирд╛рдХрд░-рджрд░реНрд╢рдирд╛рдЪрд╛рд░реНрдп рджрд░реНрд╢рдирд╡рд╛рдЪрд╕реНрдкрддрд┐-рдкреНрд░рднреГрддрд┐рдирд╛рдирд╛рд╡рд┐рд░реБрджрднрд╛рдЬрди-рдХрд▓рд┐рдХрд╛рддрд╛-рд░рд╛рдЬрдХреАрдп рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрдорд╣рд╛рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рд▓рдпрд╛рдзреНрдпрдХреНрд╖ рд╡рдЩреНрдЧреАрдпрд╕рд╕реНрдХреГрдд-рдкрд░реАрдХреНрд╖рд╛рд╕рдорд┐рддрд┐- рдордиреНрддреНрд░рд┐рдкреНрд░рд╡рд░-рд╢реНрд░реАрдпреБрдХреНрдд-рд╕реБрд░реЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдирд╛рдерджрд╛рд╕рдЧреБрдкреНрдд-рдорд╣реЛрджрдпреЗрднреНрдп

рдЙрддреНрд╕рд░реНрдЧрдкрддреНрд░рдореН

рд╣реЗ рд╡реАрд░ рдкреНрд░рддрд┐рднрд╛ рддрд╡ рдкреНрд░рддрдиреБрддреЗ рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпреЗ рд╡рд┐рджрд╛ рд╡рд┐рд╕реНрдордп
рджрд┐рд╡реНрдпрд╛ рджрд╛рд░реНрд╢рдирд┐рдХреА рдХреГрддрд┐рд╢реНрдЪ рдорд╣рддреА рдХреАрд░реНрддрд┐рд╕реНрддрд╡рд╛рд▓реМрдХрд┐рдХреАред
рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдерд╛рд╕реНрддреЗ рдХрддрд┐ рд╡рд╛ рд╡рд┐рднрд┐рдиреНрдирд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╛ рднрд┐рдиреНрджрдиреНрддрд┐ рдЪреЗрддреНрдд рддрдо
рдХрд┐ рддрддреНрддреНрд╡ рддрджрд┐рд╣рд╛рд╕реНрддрд┐ рдпрджреНрдзрд┐ рднрд╡рддреЛ рдмреБрджреНрдзреЛ рди рд╡рд┐рднреНрд░рд╛рдЬрддреЗрее

рд▓рдмреНрдзреНрд╡рд╛ рднрд╛рд░рддрд░рд╛рдЬрд╕рд╕реНрдХреГрддрдорд╣рд╛рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрд╛рд▓рдпрд╛рдзреНрдпрдХреНрд╖рддрд╛
рд╡рдЩреНрдЧреАрдпрд╛рдорд░рд╡рд╛рдХреНрдкрд░реАрдХреНрд╖рдгрд╕рднрд╛рд╕рд╛рдЪрд┐рд╡реНрдпрдорд╛рд╕реЗрджрд┐рд╡рд╛рдиреНред
рджреАрдирд╛ рджреИрд╡рддрднрд╛рд░рддреА рд╕рддрд┐рдорд┐рд░рд╛рдорд╛рд▓реЛрдХрдпрдиреНрдирд░реНрдЪреНрдЪрд┐рд╖рд╛

FOREWORD

To write a Sanskrit drama in the present day may seem to be an anachronism but we should receive, rather with a kindly eye, the last signs of life of this ancient language of our land

The present drama purports to be a n─Бtaka Whether it can be designated as a n─Б╚Ыaka from the point of view of the later theorists is doubtful However, the present drama professes to be prayog─Бnugunakrama’ The reader should judge that for himself

The idea of the plot has been taken from such sources as Adhy─Бtma R─Бm─Бyana (Ayodhy─Б K 80 ff) and theKrttiv─Бsa R─Бm─Бyana in Bengali, but it has been refashioned by the dramatist almost beyond recognition From the above sources we know that Ratn─Бkara was at first a robber and that he afterwards became a saint This nucleus has been given the present shape by the author

The dramaopens with a famine-stricken scene Ratn─Бkara the pious enters begging A whole days toil in securing alms has brought him nothing The picture of his fasting parents, wife and son moves him deeply But those who have ample food will not part with it, and the poor are left only to starve At first he makes up his mind to save his family by taking to robbing But good sense prevails, and he gives up that project Next, he determines to put an end to his miserable life

At this critical moment he finds a woman about to be molested by a robber He postpones his contemplated suicide and saves the woman by completely out-manoeuvring the robber The robber happens to be the dreadful V─лrabala, the head of a gang He is the son of a Br─Бhmana and has taken to robbery from d─▒re want He advises Ratn─Бkara to take to this profession, when he hears of his

sad plight Ratn─Бkara hesitates and then decides to join the gang as its chief, for that is the only way to save his starving family He shall destroy the miserly rich and shall uphold the starving poor The two become fast friends and prepare to fulfil their mission Ratn─Бkara saves his starving family and prepares to save others who starve (Act 1) He is to destroy the cruel king K─Бmesvara and to establish a new kingdom

Next follows a scene in the court of the king K├дme┼Ыvara bearing instances of the kings cruelty and highhandednesswhich add to the misery of the famine-stricken land A Br─Бhmana unable to bear torture curses him with utter destruction The king intent to pacify his troubled heart decides to go to Lil─Бvat─▒, his object of lust She became a widow in her early years The king fell over head and ears in love with her His love was reciprocated So the king killed her aged father and brought her into his harem Here the Second Act ends with a scene of drunken revelry (II)

Ratn─Бkara using a strategy robs the royal treasury of king K─Бmesvara The king issues orders for the immediate detection and arrest of the gang of robbers (III) The sage Cyavana, the father of Ratn─Бkara and Ratn─Бkara’s son Atreya go to pay up their debts to Dhanadatta, a merchant They owe him two hundred coins and they pay off the whole amount out of the money which Ratn─Бkara has received “from one of his loving friends who prefers to remain incognitoтАЩ But suspicion arises in the mind of one of the members of the Intelligence Branch, who was perchance present at the time of payment This leads to the arrest of the father and the son of Ratn─Бkara and as they are being thrashed so that truth might be extracted, Ratn─Бkara enters with a few men, kills the officers outright and carries away his father and his son who have fainted away He takes refuge with his whole family in the newly founded city of Ratnapura (IV)

The Fifth Act begins with a discourse between Sumat─▒ (Good Reason) and Kumat─▒ (Bad Reason) Kumat─▒ is exhilarant of her success with Ratn─Бkara while Sumat─▒ is deeply grieved but optimistic of Ratn─Бkara s ultimate revivification into piety Then we find Ratn─Бkara in Ratnapura, the refuge of all poor and oppressed people One of his comrades returns from the city of the king K─Бmesvara, as he is talking with his son Atreya This man gives an account of his activities in the city of king K─Бmesvara, of how he has arranged, with his magical arts for the murder of certain important personages He further informs that the king would be coming on a naval excursion on the river Saray┼л very soon Ratn─Бkara prepares for the capture of the king so that he may retaliate the thrashing that was meted out to his father and his son by the king’s men (V)

The Sixth Act opens with two fishermen who are busy with their baffled efforts to collect fish They retire to see the fun as the king s boat approaches The king and the woman are seen absorbed in drunken revelry on their boat, as the boats in train pass away Then comes on a boat Ratn─Бkara with his gang He prepares a strategy for the capture of the king The fishermen who had been observing all this from a concealed nook retire In horror even leaving their boat the only means of their living, behind at the very prospect of this daring feat of Ratn─Бkara Then Cyavana enters the stage with his wife Cyavana looks dejected his wife asks the reason thereof Though unwilling, he relates to her the fact that Ratn─Бkara has taken to robbery The old sage even contemplates committing suicide if that would improve his wayward son (VI)

In the Seventh Act we find Ratn─Бkara with his captive king K─Бmesvara Cyavana takes charge of the captive and the tired gang retire to rest, after having tied the king to a tree (VII) Cyavana, however sets the king free in the darkness of night, writes a letter to Ratn─Бkara to that effect

and adds that he is committing suicide so that even this may put a stop to Ratn─Бkara s ignominious activities Then he puts an end to his life by hanging himself Even before the day has dawned, Ratn─Бkara comes in exhilaration but discovers the calamity with a shock His wife, his child and his mother come, and they all faint away at this awful sight (VIII)

In the last Act we learn of the melodramatic death of Ratn─Бkara s mother, of his child and of his wife A great change has overtaken Ratn─Бkara, he sets free the recaptured king, makes arrangements so that the poor men of Ratnapura may live honestly and happily and dissolves his gang entreating the members not to take to robbery again Then he contemplates suicide, but Good Reason dissuades him and advises him to take initiation from a good preceptor N─Бrada sent from heaven initiates him into a new and pious life (IX)

The style is lucid No prakrit has been used in the drama though theory requires it Besides the common classical metres such as Anustubh, Upaj─Бti (of Indravajr─Б and Upendravajr─Б, and of Vamsastha and Indravams─Б), Vasantatilaka, ┼а─Бlin─л ┼а─Бrd┼лlav─▒kr─▒d─▒ta, etc, some novel types of j─Бti verses have been used in songs

Amareswar Thakur
Lecturer, Calcutta University
and
Secretary, Sanskrit Sahitya Parishat

INTRODUCTION

In any literature the drama is invariably regarded as the sublimest product of the poetic mind summing up in itself the essential conception of literary art It is only the drama of all forms of poetic composition that seeks to deal with the deeper questions of human life and as such may rightly be called тАШthe vital spark in literature A few centuries back, the Sanskrit literature too had been in the height of glory, and signs of its vital exuberances are still to be traced in the extant dramatic works of Asvaghosa Bh─Бsa, K─Бl─лd─Бsa, ┼Ъ┼лdraka, Harsa, Bhavabh┼лti Ksemi┼Ыvara Vis─Бkhadatta Rajasekhara and others But the necessary reaction set in and a rapid decline reduced the language of the immortals’ to such a decadent state that in recent years it came to be seriously ignored as a dead language In more modern times however a fresh laudable endeavour is being made for its resuscitation and the authorities of the Sanskrit Sahitya Parishat deserve the warmest congratulations of all lovers of classics for the valuable service they have been untiringly rendering to the cause of the revival and popularization of the Sanskrit language and literature for the last twenty-five years

Pandit Kal─▒pada Tarkacharya the author of the present work, is one of the band of energetic pioneer workers who founded the Parishat and made it what it now is He was at one time the Professor of this Institution and the Editor of the monthly Journal of the Parishat in which the present drama has been serially published He devoted the best portions of his life and energy in moulding the present batch of youthful workers and scholars of the Parishat The humble writer of this Introduction feels proud as he recalls to his mind the happy old days when he

had the unique privilege of sitting at Pandit Tarkacharya s feet to take his first lessons in Sanskrit literature

Pandit Tarkacharya belongs to a family of erudite Sanskrit scholars, famous for their versatility It is the renowned Pandit-family of Kot─Бlip─Бd─Б, Faridpur which produced, among other reputed savants, the celebrated Advaita philosopher and devotee Sri Madhusudana Sarasvati (c 16th century A D) Pandit Tarkacharya was edu cated in Bhattapalli (the well-known centre of Sanskrit learning in South west Bengal) under the paternal care of the late Mah─Бmahop─Бdhy─Бya Pandit Sivachandra S─Бrvabhauma of hallowed memory

If in his early youth Pandit Tarkacharya had worked for the renascence of Sanskrit, in his present advanced age too, he has not left off his noble habit During these years he has never been found failing in his valuable contributions to the Sanskrit literature, in the form of poems lyrics, dramas and philosophical treatises And the present drama. is the most recent of his productions

If the staging of dramas written in any language shows that the language is not yet obsolete then the Sanskrit Sahitya Parishat, which has staged no less than twenty-five Sanskrit dramas, may justly claim that Sanskrit is still a living language If, again, the normal practice of writing Sanskrit dramas in any language be any proof to its currency, then the Sanskrit dramas of Pandit Tarkacharya can certainly falsify the allegation of lifelessness usually brought against the really immortal language

The present work, which is the outcome of the mature pen and natural poetic talents of the learned author, has for its background the well known popular story about the early life of the sage V─Бlm─лki the celebrated author of the R─Бm─Бyaс╣Зa

From the versified Bengali version of the R─Бm─Бyana by the renowned Bengali Poet Krttiv─Бsa, we come to know that the revered sage V─Бlm─лk─▒ was a vile robber named

Ratn─Бkara in his early youth But his early history, as detailed in the original R─Бm─Бyana itself, contains no such reference The Adhy─Бtma R─Бm─Бyana, on the other hand seems to be the possible source of the well-known story carefully interwoven by the Bengali Poet in his vernacular Epic

In the Sanskrit source, we find thesage V─Бlm─▒k─▒ recounting before Sri R─Бmacandra the ignominious account of his past life Originally he had been a Br─Бhmana by birth, but by close associations with foresters fowlers, thieves and robbers, he gradually turned a robber himself Subsequently, however, he met seven saints whose edifying instructions produced a sanctifying influence on him He gave up his vicious mode of living and began to lead an intensely religious life The sages advised him to mutter the syllables MA R─А (=R─Бma inverted) Ages passed by and he became so intent in his devotion that his body became gradually covered all over with ant hill Yet he stirred not Long after the same seven sages, passing by the ant-hill, heard the muttering sound within it and out of pity took him out of that curious tomb As he had been entombed in an ant-hill (valmika) he came to be known as тАШV─Бlm─лk─▒’ from that day forward

It should be noted in this connexion that the Adhy─Бtma R─Бm─Бyana does not mention the name ‘Ratn─Бkara’ which seems to be an original contribution by the Bengali Poet

This resurrection of the bandit Ratn─Бkara as saint V─Бlmiki, has been hinted at in one passage of the Brahmavaivarta Pur─Бna, in the introductory portions of the com mentaries on the original R─Бm─Бyana by R─Бm─Бnuja and Govindar─Бja Similar stories of V─Бlmiki’s early life are also current in provinces other than Bengal The outline is everywhere the same with but minor differences here and there For example we may refer to the story current in Karс╣З─Бla that the robber muttered the syllables M R A(and not MA-RA) But with regard to one factor, all these

accounts agree viz, that the name Ratn─Бkara’ is nowhere to be found except in the Bengali R─Бm─Бyana of Krttiv─Бsa

Be that as it may the learned author of the present drama has adopted only the barest outline of this popular story and has completely remodelled it to suit the purposes of his drama The vicious pandit Ratn─Бkara of the traditional tale has in his hands, changed to a humane outlaw, who has taken to robbery not as a selfish profession, but with the noble mission of helping the destitute and depressed by saving them from the tyrannous oppressions of the rich and powerful Pandit Tarkacharya’s Ratn─Бkara reminds one of the notorious outlaw Robinhood of the West and presents a very close approach to real life The author therefore is sure to be successful in capturing the sympathy and admiration of the reader in favour of the hero of his drama But for all that he does not, for one single moment even, try to ustify the conduct of Ratn─Бkara from the ethical viewpoint The mission of the hero is indeed noble though the means selected by him for its fulfilment is certainly ill-advised So, in the denouement, the author unfolds the tragic scene of divine retribution for the final spiritual uplift of this noble but misguided hero The expiation of Ratn─Бkara, at first sight, may seem to be rather too drastic in comparison with all the sins he might have committed. But it should always be borne in mind that perfect peace can only be realized at the cost of the heaviest sacrifice and self-immolation pandit Ratn─Бkara is required to be immured alive, so that Saint V─Бlm─лk─▒ may be resurrected from his living tomb Pandit Tarkacharya has successfully brought out this inner significance of V─Бlmiki s metamorphosis by completely wiping out all associations of his past life

In composing the present drama, the learned author has not at every step rigidly followed the traditional principles
тАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФтАФ

* For details, vide the illuminating Sanskrit Introduction by the author himself

of Sanskrit dramaturgy According to the traditional conventions, a Sanskrit type drama (N─Бtaka) must be idealistic in spirit The hero is required by the canons of dramaturgy to be either a divine or a semi divinebeing But strangely enough, the hero ofthis drama is painted as an ordinary man of the worldswayed by passions and emotions virtues and vices In portraying his character the author has revealed the terrible internal struggles which a man of flesh and blood normally experiences under existing circumstances but which an idealist playwright would avoid to depict in his work Pandit Tarkacharyas attempts at realism fall in more with the line of the modern dramatist than with the ancient tradition of Sanskrit dramaturgy

To suit the taste of the present-day audience the author has tried to introduce in course of the dialogues and stray incidents popular ideas current in contemporary society Modern technique of introducing characters has also been profitably adopted But at the same time, the handling of the main plot, the usual introduction of the prologue, the d─▒v─▒sions into Acts and the conventional mode of composition (viz, the intermixture of lyric verses and prose dialogue) show that the auhtor has not swerved much from the traditional path The minor departures introduced here and there only emphasize the influence of the age and environments on the author, who like a man of genuine culture has not been a slave to the conventional rules of Sanskrit dramaturgy but has ever kept his mind wide open

Another striking feature of the present drama is the exclusion of Prakrit dialogues altogether from the body of the work All the characters speak Sanskrit which is indeed a novelty We can appreciate the motive of the author in banning Prakrit from his drama when we take into consideration the fact that the different Prakrit dialects ceased to be spoken languages even as early as the third or the fourth century AD Though the canonical works on

Sanskrit dramaturgy require the inclusion of Prakrit dialects in a major drama, yet by blindly following these authoritative dictates in these days no practical purpose is to be achieved Sanskrit, on the other hand, can be more easily understood than the literary Prakrits which invariably remain unintelligible even to the most cultured audience And hence the author deserves no unfavourable criticism for what may be called a happy departure from the ageold tradition

The intensity of Ratn─Бkara s expiation suggests the real tragedy but unlike the western tragedies the present drama does not leave us utterly disconsolate at its final conclusion The sublimity of the hero’s self-immolation evokes the sentiment of Quietism which is universally acknowledged as the characteristic trait in the character of the resurrected saint V─Бlm─лk─▒ Hence the title of the dramaтАФ‘Pra┼Ы─Бntaratn─Бkara’тАФis logically and dramatically significant

The character of V─Бlm─лki has been differently painted by different authors But it gives us immense pleasure to find that the portraiture of the saint by Pandit Tarkacharya has its own commendable touch of originality and at the same time fully retains the spirit of the ancient tradition

It would be out of context to say anything more in praise of the work in the body of the Introduction The praise should come, and we hope it must come, from the reading public We, therefore conclude here with the words of a great Indian dramatist, which aptly fit with the present situation-

We have now been left only with the task of requesting the audience to listen to this drama with proper attention and courtesy in consideration of its sublime subjectmatter and out of respect for its learned author”

** Ashokanath Shastri
Lecturer, Calcutta University**

рдЗрджрдорддреНрд░ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрддрдХрд╛рд▓рдорднрд┐рдзреЗрдп рдпрддреН рд╕рд╛рдореНрдкреНрд░рдд рддрддреНрддрддреНрдкреНрд░рджрд╢реЗрд╖реБ рд╕рддреНрдпрдкрд┐ рддрддреНрддрдиреНрдорд╛рдгреНрдбрд▓рд┐рдХ рд╡реНрдпрд╡рд╣рд╛рд░рд╛рдиреБрдХреВрд▓рдкреНрд░рд╛рдХреГрддрд╛рдкрднреНрд░рд╢рднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдкреНрд░рд╕рд╛рд░реЗ ………… рд╕рд╕реНрдХреГрддрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдпрд╛ рд╕рд╛рд░реНрд╡рдЬрдиреАрдирд╛рдпрд╛ рд╕рдХрд▓рджреЗрд╢рд╕рд╛рдзрд╛рд░рдгреНрдпрд╛ рдЕрдкрд┐ рдиреИрд╡ рддрд╛рд╡рд╛рдиреН рдкреНрд░рд╕рд╛рд░’ рд╕рдорд╛рджрд░реЛ рд▓реЛрдХрд╛рдпрддреЗрд╖реБрдкреНрд░рдпреЛрдЬрдиреЗрд╖реБ рд╕рдореБрдкрдпреЛрдЧреЛ рд╡рд╛ рд╡рд┐рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рдирдЧреЛрдЪрд░рддрд╛рдореНрдкреНрд░рддрд┐рдкрджреНрдпрддреЗред рддрдд рдПрд╡ рд╣рд┐ рд╕рд╛рдореНрдкреНрд░рдд рдирд╛рд╡рд┐рд░реНрднрд╡рдиреНрддрд┐ рдпрдерд╛рдХрд╛рдордореБрддреНрдХрд░реНрд╖рд╡рдиреНрдд рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпрдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдерд╛, рдирд╛рдкрд┐ рдкрд░рд┐рддреЛ рдордгреНрдбрдпрдиреНрддрд┐ рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрд╕рд╛рд╣рд┐рддреНрдпрднрд╛рдгреНрдбрд╛рдЧрд╛рд░ рдирд╡реАрдирдирд┐рдмрдиреНрдзрдХрд╛рд░рд╕рдореБрдкрд╣реГрддрд╛рдирд┐ рдирд╡реАрдирд╛рдирд┐ рдкреНрд░рд╢рд╕реНрддрд┐рднрд╛рдЬрдирд╛рдирд┐ рджрд╛рд░реНрд╢рдирд┐рдХрд╛рджрд┐рдирд┐рдмрдиреНрдзрд░рддреНрдирд╛рдирд┐ред рджреБрд░рд╛рдЧреНрд░рд╣рд╡рд╢реЗрди рд╣рдиреНрдд рдХреЗрдирдЪрд┐рджреН рд╡рд┐рд░рдЪрд┐рддреЗрд╜рдкрд┐ рдХрд╕реНрдорд┐рд╢реНрдЪрд┐рддреН рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрдирд┐рдмрдиреНрдзреЗ рджреБрд░реНрд▓рднрд╛ рдЦрд▓реБ рдХрдердЮреНрдЪрд┐рддреН рддрддреНрдкрд╛рдардкрд░рд┐рд╢реНрд░рдорд╕реНрд╡реАрдХрд░реНрддрд╛рд░реЛрд╜рдкрд┐ред рдирд┐рдмрдиреНрдзрдХрд╛рд░реИ рд╕реНрд╡реАрдпрд╡рд╕реНрддреБрдиреЛрдЧреМрд░рд╡рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рдирдкреНрд░реЗрд░рдгрдпрд╛ рд╡рд╛ рд╕реНрд╡рдХреГрддреЗ рдкреНрд░рдХрд░реНрд╖рдкрд░реАрдХреНрд╖рдгрдХрд╛рдореНрдпрдпрд╛ рд╡рд╛ рдкрд░рдкреНрд░рддрд┐рдкрд╛рджрдиреЗрдирд╛рддреНрдо-

** резрейрекрем рдмрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рдмреНрджреЗ }

рдирд╛рдЯрдХрдореН

(рдирд╛рдиреНрджреА)

рдиреАрд▓рдирд▓рд┐рдирдордЮреНрдЬреБрд▓рддрдиреБрд░рддрдиреБрдЪрд╛рдкрдзрд╛рд░реА

рдЕрдкрд┐ рдЪ

рдпрд╕реНрдпрд╛рд╕реАрдордЧреБрдгреИрд░рдзрд╛рд░рд┐ рд╕реБрддрд░рд╛ рд╢рд╛рдЦрд╛рдореГрдЧреИрд░реНрд╡рд╢реНрдпрддрд╛

** рдкрд╛рд░рд┐тАФрддрдерд╛ред **
(рдирд┐рд╖реНрдХреНрд░рд╛рдиреНрддреМ)

[ рдЗрддрд┐ рдкреНрд░рд╕реНрддрд╛рд╡рдирд╛ ]

(рддрдд рдкреНрд░рд╡рд┐рд╢рддрд┐ рднрд┐рдХреНрд╖рд╛рдХрд░реНрдкрдЯрд╢реЛрднрд┐рддрд╕реНрдХрдиреНрдзреЛ рд░рддреНрдирд╛рдХрд░)
